1015
       A    D O S YNTHETIC C ORRUPTIONS R EPRESENT T HE R EAL W ORLD ?
                                                                                                                                     NEW
1016
1017
       In their work Anonymous, they find the correlation between mean mIoU over the ACDC evalua-
       tion dataset (Sakaridis et al., 2021) and mean mIoU over each 2D Common Corruption (Hendrycks
1018
       & Dietterich, 2019) over the Cityscapes dataset (Cordts et al., 2016). We include Figure 7 from
1019
       their work here for ease of understanding. All models were trained using the training subset of the
1020   Cityscapes dataset. ACDC is the Adverse Conditions Dataset with Correspondences for Seman-
1021   tic Driving Scene Understanding captured in similar scenes are cityscapes but under four different
1022   domains: Day/Night, Rain, Snow, and Fog in the wild. ACDC is a community-used baseline for
1023   evaluating the performance of semantic segmentation methods on domain shifts observed in the
1024   wild. They find that there exists a very strong positive correlation between the two. This shows, that
1025   yes, synthetic corruptions can serve as a proxy for the real world. Unfortunately, a similar “in
       the wild” captured dataset does not exist for optical flow estimation to evaluate the effect of domain

                                                                 19
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1026
       shifts on the performance of optical flow methods. However, given that for the task of semantic
1027   segmentation, we find a very high positive correlation between the performance on real-world cor-
1028   ruptions and synthetic corruptions, it is a safe assumption that the same would hold true for optical
1029   flow estimation as well. Thus, in this work, we evaluate against synthetic 2D Common Corrup-
1030   tions (Hendrycks & Dietterich, 2019) and synthetic 3D Common Corruptions (Kar et al., 2022).
1031
1032
1033
       B     R EASONS F OR C ATEGORIZING M ETHODS T O T HEIR R ESPECTIVE
1034
             FAMILIES
1035                                                                                                            NEW
1036
       Over the years, various modifications have been proposed for DL-based optical flow estimation
1037
       methods. These can be based on the training strategy used or new architectures. However, bar-
1038   ring DIP Zheng et al. (2022) and StarFlow Godet et al. (2021) that appear to have significantly
1039   different architectures, all other optimal flow methods can be categorized into four major families:
1040   FlowNet (Dosovitskiy et al., 2015), PWC (Pyramid, Wrapping, and Cost Volume) (Sun et al., 2018),
1041   RAFT (Teed & Deng, 2020), and FlowFormer (Huang et al., 2022). Following we discuss the rea-
1042   soning for the categorization of each method considered.
1043
1044   B.1   F LOW N ET FAMILY                                                                                  NEW
1045
1046   Dosovitskiy et al. (2015) were the first to propose an end-to-end differentiable DL-based architec-
1047
       ture for optical flow estimation, FlowNet. Many further works were inspired by FlowNet, making
       changes to FlowNet to propose novel optical flow estimation methods. These methods include:
1048
1049          • FlowNet2.0 (Ilg et al., 2017): They improve upon FlowNet by changes to the schedule of
1050            training data usage, using a stacked architecture to include the warping of the second image
1051            with intermediate optical flow, and a sub-network to focus on small displacements.
1052          • LiteFlowNet (Hui et al., 2018): Compared to FlowNet2.0 they use a more effective flow in-
1053            ference approach at each pyramid level through a lightweight cascaded network. They also
1054            use a flow regularization layer to ameliorate the issue of outliers and vague flow boundaries
1055            by using a feature-driven local convolution, and they use feature warping instead of image
1056            warping. They use the same training schedule as FlowNet2.0 but they train their network
1057            stage-wise.
1058          • LiteFlowNet2 (Hui et al., 2020): They improve the accuracy and latency from LiteFlowNet
1059            by making minor architectural changes to LiteFlowNet. They follow the training schedule
1060            of FlowNet2.0 to some extent and perform stage-wise training.
1061          • LiteFlowNet3 (Hui & Loy, 2020): They further improve upon the LiteFlowNet2.0 by
1062            amending each cost vector using an adaptive modulation before the flow decoding to alle-
1063            viate the issue of outliers in the cost volume. Additionally, they replace each potentially
1064            inaccurately predicted optical flow with an accurate one from a near position through a
1065            warping of the flow field. They follow a special training schedule, first training the Lite-
1066
                FlowNet2 modules as mentioned in Hui et al. (2020), and then training the entire archi-
                tecture again with the LiteFlowNet3 modifications to LiteFlowNet2 following the training
1067
                protocol from FlowNet2.0.
1068
1069
1070   B.2   PWC FAMILY                                                                                         NEW
1071
       While still using features from different at different scales, warping, and cost volume, Sun et al.
1072
       (2018) proposed PWC-Net which with its architectural changes, presented a significant shift in
1073
       architectures from the traditional FlowNet. Sun et al. (2018) describe, “PWC-Net uses the current
1074   optical flow estimate to warp the CNN features of the second image. It then uses the warped features
1075   and features of the first image to construct a cost volume, which is processed by a CNN to estimate
1076   the optical flow.” This was faster than FlowNet2.0, easier to train, and significantly outperformed it
1077   on established benchmarks like KITTI2015 (Menze & Geiger, 2015) and MPI Sintel (Butler et al.,
1078   2012). PWC-Net uses a similar training schedule and protocol as FlowNet2.0. Many other works
1079   followed PWC-Net either changing the training strategy or making architectural changes to PWC-
       Net to further improve on i.i.d. performance. These methods include:

                                                        20
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1080
             • FastFlowNet (Kong et al., 2021): They replace the dual convolution feature pyramid
1081           in PWC-Net with the head enhanced pooling pyramid (HEPP) for enhancing the high-
1082           resolution pyramid feature and reducing model size, then, they propose center dense di-
1083           lated correlation layer (MFC) for constructing compact cost volume while keeping the
1084           large search radius. followed by shuffle block decoders (SBD) at each pyramid level to
1085           regress optical flow with significantly cheaper computation. They follow the same training
1086           protocol mentioned by FlowNet2.0.
1087
             • DICL (Wang et al., 2020): They improve upon PWC-Net by decoupling the connection
1088
               between 2D displacements and learn the matching costs at each 2D displacement hypoth-
1089
               esis independently, i.e., displacement-invariant cost learning. They apply the same 2D
1090           convolution-based matching net independently on each 2D displacement hypothesis to
1091           learn a 4D cost volume and avoid learning a 5D feature volume, thus saving computing
1092           resources. They use the same training protocol as PWC-Net and FlowNet2.0, and use the
1093           data augmentations proposed by VCN.
1094
1095
             • HD3 (Yin et al., 2019): They adapt a PWC-Net-like architecture for the decomposition of
               the discrete probability distribution instead of the feature representations allowing them to
1096
               learn probabilistic pixel correspondences in both optical flow and stereo matching. They
1097
               decompose the full match density into multiple scales hierarchically and estimate the local
1098           matching distributions at each scale conditioned on the matching and warping at coarser
1099           scales. This allows the local distributions to be composed together to form the global
1100           match density. They essentially follow the same training protocol as FlowNet2.0 while
1101           omitting some hard examples. Additionally, they use ImageNet1k (Russakovsky et al.,
1102           2015)-pre-trained weights for their pyramid feature extractor.
1103
1104
             • IRR (Hur & Roth, 2019): They take inspiration from classical energy minimization ap-
               proaches, as well as residual networks to propose an iterative residual refinement, they
1105
               show that their proposed IRR can be combined with both FlowNets and PWC-Net. In our
1106
               work, we consider their adaptation to PWC-Net as that has better i.i.d. performance. They
1107           use the same training procedure as PWC-Net but additionally set out-of-bound pixels (after
1108           applying augmentations the same as those in FlowNet2.0) as occluded.
1109
1110         • MaskFlowNet (Zhao et al., 2020): Zhao et al. (2020) apart their proposed Occlusion-
1111           Aware Feature Matching Module (OFMM) and Asymmetric Occlusion-Aware Feature
1112
               Matching Module (AsymOFMM) in PWC-Net and consists to two cascaded subnetworks
               for obtaining dual feature pyramids. Their proposed method helps them overcome the am-
1113
               biguity caused due to occlusions in images that induce inaccuracies in the flow fields during
1114
               warping. They use the same training protocol as IRR-PWC-Net. However, first, they train
1115           the MaskFlowNetS, then keep its weights frozen while training the entire MaskFlowNet.
1116           They use additional data from KITTI2015 and HD1k dataset (Kondermann et al., 2014) for
1117           fine-tuning on MPI-Sintel.
1118
1119         • MaskFlowNetS (Zhao et al., 2020): Proposed as the first stage of MaskFlowNet, Mask-
1120           FlowNetS inherits the network architecture from PWC-Net, but replaces the feature match-
1121
               ing modules (FMMs) by their proposed AsymOFMMs. They use the same training proce-
               dure as IRR-PWC-Net.
1122
1123         • ScopeFlow (Bar-Haim & Wolf, 2020): Bar-Haim & Wolf (2020) improve upon IRR-PWC-
1124           net by improving the data sampling process while testing the regularization and augmenta-
1125           tions used to mitigate the bias induced by the training protocols. They keep some aspects
1126           of the training protocols from FlowNet2.0 intact while changing a few like cropping, and
1127           regularization at different stages of the multi-phase training.
1128
             • VCN Yang & Ramanan (2019): They improve upon the 4D cost volume used by variants
1129
               of the PWC family by proposing volumetric encoder-decoder architectures that efficiently
1130           capture large receptive fields, multi-channel cost volumes that capture multi-dimensional
1131           notions of pixel similarities, and separable volumetric filtering that significantly reduces
1132           computation and parameters while preserving i.i.d. performance. They use a very similar
1133           training procedure as FlowNet2.0 and PWC-Net, however, with fewer iterations.


                                                       21
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1134
       B.3   RAFT FAMILY                                                                                        NEW
1135
1136   Teed & Deng (2020) proposed Recurrent All-Pairs Field Transforms (RAFT) to extract per-pixel
1137   features to build a multi-scale 4D correlation volume for all pairs of pixels. Here a recurrent unit
1138   is used to perform lookups on these correlation volumes. They use additional data and fine-tuning
1139   compared to FlowNet2.0. RAFT was a significant architectural change from PWC-Nets and inspired
1140   many future works that made modifications to RAFT to further improve i.i.d. performance. These
1141   methods include:
1142
             • CCMR (Jahedi et al., 2024a): They propose adapting RAFT to use attention-based mo-
1143           tion grouping concepts for multi-scale optical flow estimation. CCMR first computes
1144           global multi-scale context features and then uses them to guide the actual motion group-
1145           ing. While iterating both steps over all coarse-to-fine scales, Jahedi et al. (2024a) adapt
1146           cross-covariance image transformers to allow for an efficient realization while maintaining
1147           scale-dependent properties. They use a training procedure similar to MS-RAFT+, after the
1148           traditionally followed training procedure of FlowNet2.0, they additionally finetune on a
1149           mixed set from KITTI and Viper dataset (Richter et al., 2017).
1150         • CRAFT (Sui et al., 2022): CRAFT inherits the flow estimation pipeline of RAFT and
1151           revitalizes the correlation volume computation part with two proposed components: the
1152           Semantic Smoothing Transformer on the features from the second frame, and a Cross-
1153           Frame Attention Layer to compute the correlation volume. Sui et al. (2022) propose that
1154           these two components help suppress spurious correlations in the correlation volume. They
1155
               use the same training procedure as RAFT.
1156         • CSFlow (Shi et al., 2022): They propose, “Cross Strip Correlation module (CSC) and
1157           Correlation Regression Initialization module (CRI). CSC utilizes a striping operation across
1158           the target image and the attended image to encode global context into correlation volumes
1159
               while maintaining high efficiency. CRI is used to maximally exploit the global context for
               optical flow initialization”. They take inspiration from RAFT and adapt the multi-layer
1160
               GRU from the stereo estimation task to optical flow. They follow a training procedure very
1161
               similar to RAFT.
1162
1163
             • Flow1D (Xu et al., 2021a): They take inspiration from transformers (Bao et al., 2022) and
               propose a 1D attention operation that is first applied in the vertical direction of the target
1164
               image, and then a simple 1D correlation in the horizontal direction of the attended image to
1165
               achieve 2D correspondence modeling effect. The directions of attention and correlation can
1166           also be exchanged, resulting in two 3D cost volumes that are concatenated for optical flow
1167           regression, where they adopt RAFT’s framework to estimate the optical flow iteratively.
1168           They follow a very similar training procedure to RAFT, however for harder datasets, they
1169           use additional data for fine-tuning.
1170         • GMA (Jiang et al., 2021a): They adapt an RAFT architecture to include their proposed
1171           global motion aggregation (GMA) module, a transformer-based approach to find long-
1172           range dependencies between pixels in the first image, and perform global aggregation on
1173           the corresponding motion features. This modified RAFT architecture with a GMA further
1174           inspired other architectures and works for optical flow estimation. GMA has a very similar
1175           training procedure to RAFT.
1176         • GMFlow (Xu et al., 2022): They adapt RAFT to identify correspondences in image pairs
1177           by comparing their feature similarities. They use transformer-based modules to enhance
1178           extracted features, followed by self-attention modules for feature matching and flow prop-
1179           agation. Their feature extraction and feature upsampling modules are identical to RAFT.
1180           They follow a very similar training procedure to RAFT.
1181         • GMFlowNet (Zhao et al., 2022): They adopt the iterative update operator of RAFT as the
1182           optimization step for their proposed GMFlowNet. They use their proposed patch-based
1183           overlapping attention (POLA) instead of multi-headed self-attention of transformer blocks
1184           to extract large context features to improve the matching step. They follow a very similar
1185           training procedure to RAFT.
1186         • LCV (Khairi et al., 2024): They propose a lightweight module for learnable cost volume
1187           that adds onto RAFT to improve i.i.d. performance. For training, they initialize their
               learnable cost volume kernels to be identity and directly load the pre-trained weights from

                                                        22
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1188
               RAFT, and then they follow a similar training schedule as RAFT but with significantly
1189           fewer iterations.
1190
             • LLA-Flow (Xu et al., 2023b): They propose the local similarity aggregation for 4D cost
1191
               volume and present lightweight operations to diminish the impact of outliers caused by lack
1192
               of texture. They apply their module on RAFT to improve i.i.d. performance. They follow
1193           a very similar training procedure to RAFT.
1194
1195
             • MS-RAFT+ (Jahedi et al., 2024b): MS-RAFT adapted RAFT for combining hierarchical
               concepts at multiple scales. MS-RAFT+ builds on top of MS-RAFT by exploiting an addi-
1196
               tional finer scale for estimating the flow, which is made feasible using the on-demand cost
1197
               computation proposed by RAFT. They follow a very particular training schedule which is
1198           in parts similar to RAFT, however, due to an overhead of a number of learnable parameters,
1199           requires more data and training time.
1200
             • MatchFlow (Dong et al., 2023): They propose a different feature matching extractor
1201
               (FME) to be used with RAFT and GMA module, this proposed FME is pre-trained on
1202           a different dataset, which allows for increased i.i.d. performance due to better feature ex-
1203           traction and matching. After incorporating the pre-trained FME, the resultant MatchFlow
1204           is trained very similarly to RAFT.
1205
             • RapidFlow (Morimitsu et al., 2024a): Inspired by RAFT, Morimitsu et al. (2024a) propose
1206           Recurrent Adaptable Pyramids with Iterative Decoding. They propose a recurrent feature
1207           encoder that uses a single shared block with efficient 1D layers (NeXt1D) to generate fea-
1208           ture pyramids of variable levels. Their decoder is similar to RAFT, with a few changes
1209           inspired by SKFLow (Sun et al., 2022). They follow a very similar training procedure to
1210           RAFT.
1211         • RPKNet (Morimitsu et al., 2024b): They adapt RAFT to use their proposed Partial Kernel
1212           Convolution (PKConv) layers and Separable Large kernels (SLK). PKConv is used to pro-
1213           duce variable multi-scale features with a single shared block, while SLK is used to capture
1214           large context information with low computational cost. They follow a very similar training
1215           procedure to RAFT.
1216         • SCV (Jiang et al., 2021b): They adapt RAFT to use a sparse correlation volume instead of
1217           a dense correlation volume. They follow a very similar training procedure to RAFT.
1218
             • SeparableFlow (Zhang et al., 2021): They propose a separable cost volume module, a
1219           drop-in replacement to RAFT’s correlation cost volumes, that uses non-local aggrega-
1220           tion layers to exploit global context cues and prior knowledge, to disambiguate motions
1221           in poorly constrained ambiguous regions. They follow a training procedure the same as
1222           RAFT.
1223         • SKFlow (Sun et al., 2022): They propose using Super Kernels that allow for larger recep-
1224           tive fields allowing it to recover occluded motions. Finally, they use the non-local GMA
1225           module from GMA for optical flow estimations. They follow a similar training procedure
1226           to RAFT.
1227         • SplatFlow (Wang et al., 2024): They essentially propose to use splatting for feature match-
1228           ing in architectures like RAFT and GMA. As SplatFlow is proposed to be a multi-frame
1229           optical flow estimation method, it requires three frames at a time for training as opposed to
1230           the two frames used by RAFT and GMA. We use their modified version with GMA. This
1231           requires first loading the pr-trained weights of GMA as proposed by GMA, freezing them,
1232           and training the GPU prediction and convex upsampling networks introduced by Splat-
1233           flow. Then, all parameters are fine-tuned using dataset-specific finetuning procedures very
1234           similar to RAFT.
1235         • VideoFlow (Shi et al., 2023a): They propose a multi-frame optical flow estimation method
1236           and use the same iterative flow refinement module as other methods in the RAFT fam-
1237           ily, specifically they use the SKBlocks from SKFlow. For feature extractors, they take
1238           inspiration from FlowFormer (Huang et al., 2022) and use ImageNet1k pre-trained Twins-
1239
               SVT (Chu et al., 2021). They use three and five-image frames during training while fol-
               lowing training procedures slightly similar to RAFT.
1240
1241         • NeuFlow (Zhang et al., 2024): Inspired by GMFlow, they use transformer-based blocks
               to implement global cross-attention, however, they use Flash Attention (Dao et al., 2022)

                                                       23
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1242
                for slight speed improvements. They use a very similar upsampling module as GMFlow
1243            and RAFT. However, to obtain feature maps with finer details, they directly extract features
1244            from the original images using a CNN block, instead of using features for matching at the
1245             1 th     1 th
                16 and 8 scale like RAFT and GMFlow. They use a very similar training procedure as
1246            RAFT.
1247
1248
1249
       B.4   F LOW F ORMER FAMILY                                                                                NEW
1250
       Proposed by Huang et al. (2022), FlowFormer marks a significant shift in the architecture of optical
1251   flow estimation methods compared to the RAFT family.
1252
1253          • FlowFormer (Huang et al., 2022): It is a transformer-based neural network architecture
1254            for optical flow estimation. Huang et al. (2022) describe, FlowFormer tokenizes the 4D
1255            cost volume built from an image pair, encodes the cost tokens into a cost memory with
1256            alternate group transformer (AGT) layers in a latent space, and decodes the cost memory
1257
                via a recurrent transformer decoder with dynamic positional cost queries. The two-stage
                Twins-SVT (Chu et al., 2021) feature extractor is pre-trained on the ImageNet1k dataset.
1258
                After that, the training procedure of the entire FlowFormer is similar to RAFT’s training
1259
                procedure.
1260
1261
              • FlowFormer++ (Shi et al., 2023b): This is built upon FlowFormer to include Masked Cost
                Volume Autoencoding (MCVA) to improve the i.i.d. performance of FlowFormer by pre-
1262
                training the cost-volume encoder with a mask encoding strategy proposed by them. Flow-
1263
                Former++ requires significantly different pre-training, while the training and fine-tuning
1264            procedures are similar to RAFT.
1265
1266
1267   C     DATASET D ETAILS
1268
1269   F LOW B ENCH supports a total of four distinct optical flow datasets. Following, we describe these
1270   datasets in detail.
1271
1272   C.1   F LYING T HINGS 3D
1273
1274
       This is a synthetic dataset proposed by Mayer et al. (2016) largely used for training and evaluation of
       optical flow estimation methods. This dataset consists of 25000 stereo frames, of everyday objects
1275
       such as chairs, tables, cars, etc. flying around in 3D trajectories. The idea behind this dataset is
1276
       to have a large volume of trajectories and random movements rather than focus on a real-world
1277   application. In their work, Dosovitskiy et al. (2015) showed models trained on FlyingThings3D can
1278   generalize to a certain extent to other datasets.
1279
1280   C.2   KITTI2015
1281
1282   Proposed by Menze & Geiger (2015), this dataset is focused on the real-world driving scenario.
1283   It contains a total of 400 pairs of image frames, split equally for training and testing. The image
1284   frames were captured in the wild while driving around on the streets of various cities. The ground-
1285   truth labels were obtained by an automated process.
1286
1287   C.3   MPI S INTEL
1288
       Proposed by Butler et al. (2012) and Wulff et al. (2012), this dataset is derived from an open-source
1289
       animated short film and consists of a total of 1064 synthetic frames for training and 564 synthetic
1290
       frames for testing, both at a resolution of 1024 × 436. The intention of this dataset is to enforce
1291   realism while having a dataset at scale. This dataset is provided as two datasets, which are passes
1292   with more transformations and effects on the frames that originally have constant albedo over time,
1293   these passes are,
1294
1295          • MPI Sintel (clean): This is the clean pass that adds some realism to the images by adding
                some spectral effects, like illumination, shadows, and smooth shading.

                                                         24
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1296
              • MPI Sintel (final): This is the final pass that adds more realism by adding effects such as
1297            blur due to depth and camera focus, blur due to motion and atmospheric effects such as
1298            snow during snow storms, etc.
1299
1300   C.4   S PRING
1301
1302   Similar to MPI Sintel, Mehl et al. (2023) proposed a new dataset and benchmark for optical flow
1303   estimation which is much larger than any other dataset before. It consists of frames from the open-
1304   source Blender movie “Spring” and consists of 6000 stereo image pairs from 47 sequences with
1305   SotA visual effects at full HD resolution (1920 × 1080 pixels).
1306
1307   D     I MPLEMENTATION D ETAILS O F T HE B ENCHMARK
1308
1309   Following we provide details regarding the experiments done for creating the benchmark used in the
1310   analysis.
1311
1312   Compute Resources. Most experiments were done on a single 40 GB NVIDIA Tesla V100 GPU
1313   each, however, MS-RAFT+, FlowFormer, and FlowFormer++ are more compute-intensive, and thus
1314   80GB NVIDIA A100 GPUs or NVIDIA H100 were used for these models, a single GPU for each
1315   experiment.
1316
1317
       Datasets Used.       Performing adversarial attacks and OOD robustness evaluations are very ex-
1318
       pensive and compute-intensive. Thus, performing evaluation using all model-dataset pairs is not
1319
       possible given the limited computing resources at our disposal. Thus, for the benchmark, we only
1320   use KITTI2015, MPI Sintel (clean), and MPI Sintel (final) as these are the most commonly used
1321   datasets for evaluation (Ilg et al., 2017; Huang et al., 2022; Schmalfuss et al., 2022b; Schrodi et al.,
1322   2022; Agnihotri et al., 2024).
1323
1324
       Metrics Calculation.        In Sec. 4 we introduce three new metrics for better understanding our
1325
       analysis, given the large scale of the benchmark created. For calculating TARE and NARE values
1326                                                                                              8
       we used BIM, PGD, and CosPGD attack with step size α=0.01, perturbation budget ϵ = 255        under
1327   the ℓ∞ -norm bound, as targeted and non-targeted attacks respectively. We use ℓ∞ -norm bound as
1328   we observe in Appendix H that there is a high correlation between the performance of optical flow
1329   estimation methods when attacked using ℓ∞ -norm bounded attacks and ℓ2 -norm bounded attacks.
1330   We use 20 attack iterations for calculating TARE and NARE as we observe in Appendix H,
1331   that at a lower number of iterations, the gap in performance of different optical flow estimation
1332   methods is small, thus an in-depth analysis would be difficult, and we do not go beyond 20 attack
1333   iterations as computing each attack step for an adversarial attack is very expensive, and as shown
1334   by Agnihotri et al. (2024) and Schmalfuss et al. (2022b), 20 iterations are enough to optimize an
1335
       attack to truly understand the performance of the attacked method. For calculating GAE, we use
       all 15 2D Common Corruptions: ‘Gaussian Noise’, Shot Noise’, ‘Impulse Noise’, ‘Defocus Blur’,
1336
       ‘Frosted Glass Blur’, ‘Motion Blur’, ‘Zoom Blur’, ‘Snow’, ‘Frost’, ‘Fog’, ‘Brightness’, ‘Contrast’,
1337
       ‘Elastic Transform’, ‘Pixelate’, ‘JPEG Compression’, and eight 3D Common Corruptions: ‘Color
1338   Quantization’, ‘Far Focus’, ‘Fog 3D’, ‘ISO Noise’, ‘Low Light’, ‘Near Focus’, ‘XY Motion Blur’,
1339   and ‘Z Motion Blur’. All the common corruptions are at severity 3. Kar et al. (2022) offers more
1340   3D Common Corruptions, however computing them is resource intensive. Thus, given our limited
1341   resources and an overlap in the corruptions between 2D Common Corruptions and 3D Common
1342   Corruptions, we focus on generating 3D Common Corruptions that might be unique from their 2D
1343   counterpart, require fewer sources to generate, and are interesting from an optical flow estimation
1344   perspective.
1345   Calculating the EPE. EP E is the Euclidean distance between the two vectors, where one vector
1346   is the predicted flow by the optical flow estimation method and the other vector is the ground truth
1347   in case of i.i.d. performance evaluations, non-targeted attacks evaluations, and OOD robustness
1348   evaluations, while it is the target flow vector, in case of targeted attacks. For each dataset, the EP E
1349   value is calculated over all the samples of the evaluation set of the respective dataset and then the
       mean EP E value is used as the mean-EP E of the respective method over the respective dataset.             NEW

                                                         25
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1350
       Other Metrics. Apart from EPE, F LOW B ENCH also enables calculating a lot of other interesting
1351   metrics, such as ℓ0 , ℓ2 , ℓ∞ , distance between the perturbations of each image before and after a
1352   threat. Apart from these, in all scenarios, we also capture the outlier error, 1-px error, 3-px error,
1353   5-px error and cosine distance between two vectors. These vectors are the same as that in the case of
1354   EP E calculations. We limited the analysis in this work to use EP E, since it is the most commonly
1355   used metric for evaluation, moreover, most works on optical flow estimation (Agnihotri et al., 2024;
1356   Schmalfuss et al., 2022b; Schrodi et al., 2022; Teed & Deng, 2020; Jahedi et al., 2024b) show a very
1357   high correlation between performance evaluations using different metrics.
1358
1359   Models Used.      All available checkpoints, as shown in Tab. 1 for MPI Sintel and KITTI2015
1360   dataset were used for creating the benchmark, except the following four models: Separableflow,
1361   SCV, VCN, Unimatch as due to special operations used in these models, they required specific
1362   libraries which were creating conflicts with all the others models, and as most of these models are
1363   very old and do not have performance close to SotA performance, we did not include them.
1364
1365
       Adversarial Weather For generating adversarial weather attacks, we followed the implementa-
1366
       tion proposed by Schmalfuss et al. (2023). However, generating this attack is highly compute-
1367   intensive, and thus doing so for all models was not possible. Thus, based on the performance and
1368   reliability of all the models, we identified a few (eight) interesting models and only attacked them
1369   using the four different attacks curtailed within adversarial weather. This was done to demonstrate
1370   the capability of F LOW B ENCH to perform this attack. The following are the specifications for the
1371   weather attacks:
1372
1373          • Adversarial Weather: Snow (random snowflakes)
1374              – Number of Particles: 3000
1375              – Number of optimization steps: 750
1376
              • Adversarial Weather: Rain (rain streaks of length 0.15 with motion blur )
1377
1378              – Number of Particles: 20
1379              – Number of optimization steps: 750
1380          • Adversarial Weather: Fog (random large less opacity particles)
1381              – Number of Particles: 60
1382
                  – Number of optimization steps: 750
1383
1384          • Adversarial Weather: Sparks (random red sparks)
1385              – Number of Particles: 3000
1386              – Number of optimization steps: 750
1387
1388   Please note, that these specifications are identical to the optimal ones proposed by Schmalfuss et al.
1389   (2023).
1390
1391   E    D ESCRIPTION OF F LOW B ENCH
1392
1393   Following, we describe the benchmarking tool, F LOW B ENCH. It is built using pltflow (Morimitsu,
1394   2021), and supports 36 unique architectures and 4 distinct datasets, namely FlyingThings3D (Mayer
1395   et al., 2016), KITTI2015 (Menze & Geiger, 2015), MPI Sintel (Butler et al., 2012) (clean and fi-
1396   nal) and Spring (Mehl et al., 2023) datasets (please refer Appendix C for additional details on the
1397   datasets). It enables training and evaluations on all aforementioned datasets including evaluations
1398   using SotA adversarial attacks such as CosPGD (Agnihotri et al., 2024) and PCFA (Schmalfuss
1399   et al., 2022b), Adversarial weather (Schmalfuss et al., 2023), and other commonly used adversar-
1400
       ial attacks like BIM (Kurakin et al., 2018), PGD (Kurakin et al., 2017), FGSM (Goodfellow et al.,
       2015), under various lipshitz (lp ) norm bounds.
1401
1402   Additionally, it enables evaluations for Out-of-Distribution (OOD) robustness by corrupting the in-
1403   ference samples using 2D Common Corruptions (Hendrycks & Dietterich, 2019) and 3D Common
       Corruptions (Kar et al., 2022).

                                                        26
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1404
       We follow the nomenclature set by RobustBench (Croce et al., 2021) and use “threat model” to
1405   define the kind of evaluation to be performed. When “threat model” is defined to be “None”, the
1406   evaluation is performed on unperturbed and unaltered images, if the “threat model” is defined to
1407   be an adversarial attack, for example “PGD”, “CosPGD” or “PCFA”, then F LOW B ENCH performs
1408   an adversarial attack using the user-defined parameters. We elaborate on this in Appendix E.1.
1409   Whereas, if “threat model” is defined to be “2DCommonCorruptions” or “3DCommonCorruptions”,
1410   the F LOW B ENCH performs evaluations after perturbing the images with 2D Common Corruptions
1411   and 3D Common Corruptions respectively. We elaborate on this in Appendix E.2.
1412   If the queried evaluation already exists in the benchmark provided by this work, then F LOW B ENCH
1413   simply retrieves the evaluations, thus saving computation.
1414
1415   E.1   A DVERSARIAL ATTACKS
1416
1417   F LOW B ENCH enables the use of all the attacks mentioned in Sec. 2.3 to help users better study the
1418   reliability of their optical flow methods. We choose to specifically include these white-box adver-
1419   sarial attacks as they either serve as the common benchmark for adversarial attacks in classification
1420   literature (FGSM, BIM, PGD, APGD) or they are unique attacks proposed specifically for pixel-wise
1421
       prediction tasks (CosPGD) and optical flow estimation (PCFA and Adversarial Weather). These at-
       tacks can either be Non-targeted which are designed to simply fool the model into making incorrect
1422
       predictions, irrespective of what the model eventually predicts, or can be Targeted, where the model
1423
       is fooled to make a certain prediction. Most attacks can be, designed to be either Targeted or Non-
1424   targeted, these include, FGSM, BIM, PGD, APGD, CosPGD and Adversarial Weather. However, by
1425   design, some attacks are limited to being only one of the two, for example, PCFA which is a targeted
1426   attack. Following, we discuss these attacks in detail and highlight their key differences.
1427
1428
       FGSM. Assuming a non-targeted attack, given a model fθ and an unperturbed input sample
1429
       X clean and ground truth label Y , FGSM attack adds noise δ to X clean as follows,
1430
1431
1432                          X adv = X clean + α · sign∇X clean L(fθ (X clean ), Y ),                    (1)
1433
1434
                                            δ = ϕϵ (X adv − X clean ),                                    (2)
1435
1436
1437                                         X adv = ϕr (X clean + δ).                                    (3)
1438   Here, L(·) is the loss function (differentiable at least once) which calculates the loss between the
1439   model prediction and ground truth, Y . α is a small value of ϵ that decides the size of the step to
1440   be taken in the direction of the gradient of the loss w.r.t. the input image, which leads to the input
1441   sample being perturbed such that the loss increases. X adv is the adversarial sample obtained after
1442   perturbing X clean . To make sure that the perturbed sample is semantically indistinguishable from
1443   the unperturbed clean sample to the human eye, steps from Eq. (2) and Eq. (3) are performed. Here,
1444   function ϕϵ is clipping the δ in ϵ-ball for ℓ∞ -norm bounded attacks or the ϵ-projection in other
1445
       lp -norm bounded attacks, complying with the ℓ∞ -norm or other lp -norm constraints, respectively.
       While function ϕr clips the perturbed sample ensuring that it is still within the valid input space.
1446
       FGSM, as proposed, is a single step attack. For targeted attacks, Y is the target and α is multiplied
1447
       by -1 so that a step is taken to minimize the loss between the model’s prediction and the target
1448   prediction.
1449
1450
1451   BIM. This is the direct extension of FGSM into an iterative attack method. In FGSM, X clean was
1452
       perturbed just once. While in BIM, X clean is perturbed iteratively for time steps t ∈ [0, T ], such
       that t ∈ Z+ , where T are the total number of permissible attack iterations. This changes the steps
1453
       of the attack from FGSM to the following,
1454

1480   Figure 8: Examples of MPI Sintel images perturbed by the mentioned adversarial attacks and the
1481
       optical flow predictions using FlowFormer++. These examples are intended to show the versatility
       of F LOW B ENCH.
1482
1483
1484   Here, at t=0, X advt =X clean .
1485
1486   PGD. Since in BIM, the initial prediction always started from X clean , the attack required a sig-
1487   nificant amount of steps to optimize the adversarial noise and yet it was not guaranteed that in the
1488   permissible ϵ-bound, X advt+1 was far from X clean . Thus, PGD proposed introducing stochasticity
1489   to ensure random starting points for attack optimization. They achieved this by perturbing X clean
1490   with U(−ϵ, ϵ), a uniform distribution in [−ϵ, ϵ], before making the first prediction, such that, at t=0
1491                                     X advt = ϕr (X clean + U(−ϵ, ϵ)).                                (7)
1492
1493
1494   APGD. Auto-PGD is an effective extension to the PGD attack that effectively scales the step size
1495   α over attack iterations considering the compute budget and the success rate of the attack.
1496
1497
       CosPGD. All previously discussed attacks were proposed for the image classification task. Here,
1498   the input sample is a 2D image of resolution H × W, where H and W are the height and width of the
1499   spatial resolution of the sample, respectively. Pixel-wise information is inconsequential for image
1500   classification. This led to the pixel-wise loss L(·) being aggregated to L(·), as follows,
1501                                                    1      X
1502                          L(fθ (X advt ), Y ) =                 L(fθ (X advt )i , Yi ).            (8)
                                                    H×W
1503                                                          i∈H×W

1504   This aggregation of L(·) fails to account for pixel-wise information available in tasks other than
1505   image classification, such as pixel-wise prediction tasks like Optical Flow estimation. Thus, in their
1506   work Agnihotri et al. (2024) propose an effective extension of the PGD attack that takes pixel-wise
1507   information into account by scaling L(·) by the alignment between the distribution of the predictions
1508
       and the distributions of Y before aggregating leading to a better-optimized attack, modifying Eq. (4)
       as follows,
1509                                             X
        X advt+1 = X advt + α · sign∇X advt            cos ψ(fθ (X advt )i ), Ψ(Yi ) · L fθ (X advt )i , Yi .

1510
1511                                           i∈H×W
                                                                                                          (9)

                                                         28
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1512
       Where, functions ψ and Ψ are used to obtain the distribution over the predictions and Yi , respec-
1513   tively, and the function cos calculates the cosine similarity between the two distributions. CosPGD
1514   is the unified SotA adversarial attack for pixel-wise prediction tasks.
1515
1516
1517   PCFA. Recently proposed by Schmalfuss et al. (2022b), is the SotA targeted adversarial at-
1518
       tack specifically designed for optical flow estimation. It optimizes the input perturbation δ =
       X advt − X clean within a given l2 bound to obtain a given target flow Y targ . Mathematically,
1519
       PCFA transforms the constrained optimization problem to find the most destructive perturbation un-
1520
       der an l2 constraint ε2 into an unconstrained optimization problem by adding a term that penalizes
1521   deviations from the l2 constraint:
1522                                                                               √
1523    X advt+1 = X advt + argmin(L(fθ (X advt ), Y targ ) + µ · ReLU(∥δ̂∥22 − (ε2 2 × H × W )2 )) (10)
                                              δ̂
1524
1525   Here, L(·) is a generic loss function, like EPE or cosine distance. The penalty scaling parameter µ
1526   influences how severely deviations from the per-pixel l2 bound ε2 are penalized. The optimization
1527   problem argmin(·) is solved with an L-BFGS optimizer.
                         δ̂
1528
1529
1530   Adversarial Weather. Unlike the previous attacks which introduced per-pixel modifications, ad-
1531   versarial weather Schmalfuss et al. (2023; 2022a) attacks optical flow methods through optimizing
1532   the motion trajectories of rendered weather particles P like snow flakes, rain drops or fog clouds.
1533   The particle trajectories are modelled as positions P = {P1 , P2 } in the two frames I1 , I2 . Con-
1534
       sequently, X adv (P ) is generated by differentiably rendering the particles with their respective 3D
       positions to the 2D images. The update step optimizes the particle positions to achieve a certain
1535
       target flow Y targ while simultaneously limiting the position offset size δP t = P init − P t :
1536
                                                                                                           j
1537                                                                                              X βI X ∥δP t ∥22
             adv        t+1         adv            t                   adv     t        targ
1538    X          (P         )=X             P + α · ∇P t EPE(fθ (X         (P )), Y          )+            I
                                                                                                                      .
1539
                                                                                                    |P|
                                                                                                 I∈1,2
                                                                                                           djI
                                                                                                         j∈P
1540                                                                                                  (11)
1541
       Here, β balances the two optimization goals of reaching the target flow and limiting trajectory off-
       sets. The allowed trajectory offsets are further scaled with the particle depth d in the scene, to
1542
       generate visually pleasing results.
1543
1544   Fig. 8, shows adversarial examples created using the SotA attacks and how they affect the model
1545   predictions.
1546
1547   E.2     O UT- OF -D ISTRIBUTION ROBUSTNESS
1548
1549
       While adversarial attacks help explore vulnerabilities of inefficient feature representations learned by
       a model, another important aspect of reliability is generalization ability. Especially, generalization
1550
       to previously unseen samples or samples from significantly shifted distributions compared to the
1551
       distribution of the samples seen while learning model parameters. As one cannot cover all possible
1552   scenarios during model training, a certain degree of generalization ability is expected from models.
1553   However, multiple works (Hendrycks & Dietterich, 2019; Kar et al., 2022; Hoffmann et al., 2021)
1554   showed that models are surprisingly less robust to distribution shifts, even those that can be caused
1555   by commonly occurring phenomena such as weather changes, lighting changes, etc. This makes the
1556   study of Out-of-Distribution (OOD) robustness an interesting avenue for research. Thus, to facilitate
1557   the study of robustness to such commonly occurring corruptions, F LOW B ENCH enables evaluating
1558   against prominent image corruption methods. Following, we describe these methods in detail.
1559
1560   2D Common Corruptions. Hendrycks & Dietterich (2019) propose introducing distribution shift
1561   in the input samples by perturbing images with a total of 15 synthetic corruptions that could occur
1562   in the real world. These corruptions include weather phenomena such as fog, and frost, digital
1563   corruptions such as jpeg compression, pixelation, and different kinds of blurs like motion, and zoom
1564   blur, and noise corruptions such as Gaussian and shot noise amongst others corruption types. Each
1565   of these corruptions can perturb the image at 5 different severity levels between 1 and 5. The final
       performance of the model is the mean of the model’s performance on all the corruptions, such that

                                                                29
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1566       Color Quantization           Far Focus                  Fog 3D                  ISO Noise
1567
1568
1569
1570           Low Light                Near Focus            XY-Motion blur              Z-Motion Blur
1571
1572
1573
1574   Figure 9: Examples of images from KITTI2015 corrupted using 3D Common Corruptions for eval-
1575   uation of OOD robustness.
1576
1577
1578   every corruption is used to perturb each image in the evaluation dataset. Since these corruptions are
1579
       applied to a 2D image, they are collectively termed 2D Common Corruptions.
1580
1581   3D Common Corruptions.         Since the real world is 3D, Kar et al. (2022) extend 2D Common
1582   Corruptions to formulate more realistic-looking corruptions by leveraging depth information (syn-
1583   thetic depth information when real depth is not readily available) and luminescence angles. They
1584   name these image corruptions as 3D Common Corruptions. Fig. 9, shows examples of KITTI2015
1585   images corrupted using 3D Common Corruptions.
1586
1587   F     M ODEL Z OO
1588
1589   The trained checkpoints for all models available in F LOW B ENCH can be obtained using the follow-
1590   ing lines of code:
1591
1592         from flowbench.evals import load_model
1593         model = load_model(model_name='RAFT', dataset='KITTI2015')
1594
       Each model checkpoint can be retrieved with the pair of ‘model name’, the name of the model,
1595
       and ‘dataset’, the dataset for which the checkpoint was last fine-tuned. In Table 1, we provide a
1596   comprehensive look-up table for all ‘model name’ and ‘dataset’ pairs for which trained checkpoints
1597   are available in FlowBench.                                                                             NEW

1706            string.

1710            sive).

1712

1713

1717   F LOW B ENCH supports the following 2D Common Corruption: ‘gaussian noise’, shot noise’, ‘im-
1718   pulse noise’, ‘defocus blur’, ‘frosted glass blur’, ‘motion blur’, ‘zoom blur’, ‘snow’, ‘frost’, ‘fog’,
1719   ‘brightness’, ‘contrast’, ‘elastic’, ‘pixelate’, ‘jpeg’. For the evaluation, F LOW B ENCH will evaluate
1720   the model on the validation images from the respective dataset corrupted using each of the afore-
1721
       mentioned corruptions for the given severity, and then report the mean performance over all of them.

                                                                                                                                                                                                           NEW
2732
2733   In Figure 83 we share a screenshot from our prototype website currently under work, that would
2734
       help better understand the metrics. In this screenshot, the methods are ranked based on their EPE
       w.r.t. the ground truth flow under non-targeted CosPGD attack at 20 attack iterations under the ℓ∞ -
2735
       norm bound (lower means the method is more robust) evaluated using the KITTI2015 dataset. We
2736
       are currently designing it to make the numbers and column headings better visible to the users, and
2737   the users can dynamically rank these based on any of the columns. We will host the website after
2738   acceptance.

2740
2741   J   L IMITATIONS
2742
2743   Benchmarking optical flow estimation methods is a compute and labor-intensive endeavor. Thus,
2744   best utilizing available resources we use F LOW B ENCH to benchmark a limited number of set-
2745
       tings. The benchmarking tool itself offers significantly more combinations that can be benchmarked.
       Nonetheless, the benchmark provided is comprehensive and instills interest to further utilize F LOW-
2746
       B ENCH.
2747
2748
2749   K    R EPRODUCIBILITY O F E VALUATIONS
2750
                                                                                                                                                                                                           NEW
2751   There always exists stochasticity when evaluating adversarial attacks, due to the randomness these
2752   attacks exploit, and also common corruptions due to variations in seeds and calculation approxima-
2753   tions made by various python libraries. Therefore, for transparency and reproducibility, we evaluate
       different runs on the same seed and different runs of different seeds. We report these evaluations

2783
2784
2785   here, using Tab. 2 for adversarial attacks and Tab. 3 for common corruptions, and observe that the
2786
       variance is extremely low and the analysis performed in this work still stands.
2787   To ensure the reproducibility of our adversarial attack evaluations we repeat experiments in two
2788   ways: first, three different runs with the same seed, and second, one run each for three different
2789   seeds. We observe very minute variations in results in both cases which can be attributed to cal-
2790   culation approximations made by different libraries such as pytorch (Paszke et al., 2019). Due to
2791   the compute-hungry nature of these evaluations, we limit them to using one method: RAFT on the
2792
       KITTI2015 dataset, and the attack used is CosPGD. We evaluate multiple settings: different ℓp -norm
       bounds, different attack optimization methods (optimizing w.r.t. ground truth flow and optimizing
2793
       w.r.t. initial flow prediction.), and for targeted attacks, two different targets. The attack settings are
2794
       consistent with the paper.

3237

3253   Table 2: To ensure reproducibility of our adversarial attack evaluations we repeat experiments in
3254   two ways: first, three different runs with the same seed, and second, one run each for three different
3255   seeds. We observe very minute variations in results in both cases which can be attributed to cal-
3256   culation approximations made by different libraries such as pytorch (Paszke et al., 2019). Due to
3257   the compute-hungry nature of these evaluations, we limit them to using one method: RAFT on the
3258   KITTI2015 dataset, and the attack used is CosPGD. We evaluate multiple settings: different ℓp -norm
3259   bounds, different attack optimization methods (optimizing w.r.t. ground truth flow and optimizing
3260   w.r.t. initial flow prediction.), and for targeted attacks, two different targets. The attack settings are
3261
       consistent with the paper. Target ‘None’ means the attack was Non-targeted.
